import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 3D transposed convolution operation with asymmetric input and kernel, and optional stride.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple of ints): Size of the convolution kernel in the form (kernel_size_depth, kernel_size_height, kernel_size_width).
        stride (tuple of ints, optional): Stride of the convolution in the form (stride_depth, stride_height, stride_width). Defaults to (1, 1, 1).
        padding (tuple of ints, optional): Padding applied to the input in the form (padding_depth, padding_height, padding_width). Defaults to (0, 0, 0).
        output_padding (tuple of ints, optional): Additional size added to one side of the output shape. Defaults to (0, 0, 0).
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: tuple = (1, 1, 1), padding: tuple = (0, 0, 0), output_padding: tuple = (0, 0, 0), groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose3d = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 3D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, depth_out, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose3d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 32
kernel_size = (3, 5, 7)
depth = 12
height = 24
width = 48
stride = (2, 2, 2)
padding = (1, 2, 3)
output_padding = (1, 1, 1)
groups = 4

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, groups]

reasoning
We need to implement a custom CUDA kernel that replaces PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d. The reference is a ConvTranspose3d with groups, asymmetric kernel, stride, padding, output_padding, optional bias. We need to write ModelNew class, matching constructor signature. Inside __init__, we need create weight and optionally bias parameters. Then forward will call custom CUDA kernel via torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline. We need to be careful: We have to match PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d behavior exactly. That includes the reverse of conv3d: ConvTranspose3d is effectively a dilated convolution on upsampled input, or backward of conv3d. One approach: Use the "im2col + GEMM" style for transposed convolution. Specifically, for each group: we have in_channels_per_group = in_channels // groups, out_channels_per_group = out_channels // groups. Input shape: (N, C_in, D_in, H_in, W_in) Weight shape: (C_in, C_out_per_group, Kd, Kh, Kw) because ConvTranspose3d weight in PyTorch is stored as (in_channels, out_channels//groups, kD, kH, kW). We want output shape: (N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out) where D_out = (D_in - 1)*stride_d - 2*pad_d + dilation_d*(Kd-1) + output_pad_d + 1 Similarly for H, W. With dilation=1. One standard way to implement transposed convolution is: 1. For each output sample, initialize output tensor to zeros. 2. The transposed convolution is equivalent to convolving the input with the weight transposed spatially (i.e., rotate 180 degrees for each spatial dimension? Actually the gradient w.r.t input of a standard conv uses the forward weight rotated 180). But PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d applies the weight directly as a transposed convolution: the operation can be computed as: - Consider each input element x[n, c_in, i, j, k]. - It contributes to a patch of output of size (Kd, Kh, Kw) via the weight slice w[c_in, :, :, :, :]. - Specifically, for each output channel c_out in the group, the output at spatial locations (i*stride_d + a, j*stride_h + b, k*stride_w + c) gets += x[n,c_in,i,j,k] * w[c_in, c_out, a, b, c], minus padding offset. - Need to account for padding and output_padding. Wait, let's derive the exact formula. Standard conv3d: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, d*stride_d + kd - pad_d, h*stride_h + kh - pad_h, w*stride_w + kw - pad_w] * weight[c_out, c_in//groups, kd, kh, kw] + bias[c_out]. (For groups, weight shape: (out_channels, in_channels//groups, Kd, Kh, Kw)) Transposed conv is essentially the adjoint: given input x of shape (N, C_in, D_in, H_in, W_in), compute y such that if you convolve y with the same weight (as forward conv) using stride, padding, you get x (ignoring output_padding). More formally, the transposed convolution corresponds to the gradient of conv w.r.t its input. The gradient w.r.t input of conv3d is computed by convolving the output gradient with the weight rotated 180° (flip all spatial dims) using stride=1, dilation=1, and padding computed appropriately. But PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d uses the weight stored as (C_in, C_out//groups, Kd, Kh, Kw) and the operation is equivalent to: - input is conceptually upsampled by inserting stride-1 zeros between elements. - Then apply a standard conv3d with kernel flipped? Wait, there are two equivalent views: a) Upsample input with stride (insert zeros), then pad it with kernel_size-1 - padding (this is the "full" convolution) and convolve with the weight as-is (no flipping) to get output. b) Direct accumulation: for each input element, add its value times the weight to the output patch. Because weight is not flipped in the stored parameters for ConvTranspose3d (the stored weight is defined such that the operation is the transpose of conv3d with the same weight orientation as in conv3d? Actually in PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels//groups, kH, kW). And the operation can be seen as convolving the upsampled input with the weight, but without flipping? Let's recall: For ConvTranspose2d, if you call it with stride=1 and padding=0, it's equivalent to a full convolution (i.e., output is larger). Many implementations do: output = conv_transpose2d(input, weight, ...) which is equivalent to: for each input element, scatter-add weight * input element into output. No flipping of weight is needed because the weight parameters are already defined to produce that effect. But is there a flip? In the backward of conv2d, the gradient wrt input requires flipping the kernel (rot180). However, the weight stored in ConvTranspose2d is different from Conv2d weight; it's already oriented such that conv_transpose2d(input, weight, ...) works as transposed convolution. If we call ConvTranspose2d with the same parameters as Conv2d, it's the "adjoint" operator. But the weight tensors are independent. So for our custom kernel, we can implement it as direct accumulation: y = zeros(N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out) For each n, c_in, i, j, k in input: group = c_in // in_channels_per_group For c_out_local in 0..C_out_per_group-1: c_out = group * C_out_per_group + c_out_local For kd in 0..Kd-1: for kh in 0..Kh-1: for kw in 0..Kw-1: d_out = i * stride_d + kd - pad_d h_out = j * stride_h + kh - pad_h w_out = k * stride_w + kw - pad_w if 0 <= d_out < D_out and similarly for h_out, w_out: y[n, c_out, d_out, h_out, w_out] += x[n, c_in, i, j, k] * weight[c_in, c_out_local, kd, kh, kw] After accumulation, add bias if present. Wait, does output_padding affect this? Output padding adds extra size to one side of the output shape. Typically output_padding = (out_pad_d, out_pad_h, out_pad_w) means the output size is increased by that amount relative to the formula without output_padding. But the extra padding is added at the end (i.e., the right/bottom/back side). In the accumulation formula, the valid range of output indices is 0..D_out-1, where D_out includes output_padding. However, the accumulation from input only fills the first D_out - output_pad_d entries in depth, etc. The extra tail remains zero. So we just allocate the larger size and only write into the first part. The extra tail automatically stays zero (if we zero-initialize). So we just need to compute D_out using the formula including output_padding. But is there an offset due to output_padding in the accumulation? In standard transposed conv, output_padding just enlarges the output and does not shift the data. The extra padded elements are at the end of each spatial dimension. So yes, we just compute output shape accordingly and only accumulate within the non-padded region. However, we need to be careful: The standard formula for D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride_d - 2*pad_d + dilation_d*(Kd-1) + output_pad_d + 1. Wait, there is a "- 2*pad_d" term because padding in conv_transpose3d corresponds to removing padding from the conv (i.e., the " transposed" operation). For ConvTranspose3d, the output size formula is: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride_d - 2*pad_d + dilation_d*(Kd - 1) + output_pad_d + 1 But in terms of the accumulation formula above: we have d_out = i * stride_d + kd - pad_d. Let's test: if D_in = 1, stride=1, pad=0, Kd=3. Then D_out = (1-1)*1 - 0 + (3-1) + 0 + 1 = 3. Our formula yields d_out = 0 + kd - 0 = kd ∈ {0,1,2}. Good. If D_in = 2, stride=1, pad=1, Kd=3. D_out = (2-1)*1 - 2*1 + 2 + 1 = 1 -2 +2 +1 = 2. Input indices i ∈ {0,1}. For i=0: kd ∈ {0,1,2}, d_out = kd - 1 ∈ {-1,0,1}. Only 0,1 valid. For i=1: d_out = 1+kd-1 = kd ∈ {0,1,2}. So output indices covered: 0,1,2. But D_out should be 2. Hmm, does index 2 get covered? For i=1, kd=2 => d_out=2. So D_out should be 3? Let's recompute formula: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride_d - 2*pad_d + dilation*(Kd - 1) + output_pad_d + 1. With D_in=2, stride=1, pad=1, Kd=3: (2-1)*1 - 2*1 + (3-1) + 0 + 1 = 1 - 2 + 2 + 1 = 2. Wait, index 2 would be out of bounds if D_out=2. But according to accumulation, i=1, kd=2 gives d_out=2. Is that actually part of the transposed conv? Let's check manually: For a Conv3d with stride=1, pad=1, Kd=3, input size D_out, output size D_in. We want D_out such that conv3d(output, weight, stride=1, pad=1) gives input of size 2. Conv3d output size = floor((D_out + 2*pad - Kd)/stride) + 1 = D_out + 2 - 3 + 1 = D_out. Wait, that gives D_out = D_in. That would mean D_out = 2. Let's verify: conv3d with pad=1, kernel=3, stride=1 on input size 2: output size = floor((2 + 2*1 - 3)/1) + 1 = floor(1) + 1 = 2. So D_out=2. Now transposed conv should map input size 2 to output size 2. Let's see: input indices i=0 and 1. For i=0, kernel positions kd relative to output: output index = i + kd - pad = kd - 1. For kd=0 => -1 (invalid), kd=1 => 0, kd=2 => 1. For i=1: output = 1 + kd - 1 = kd. kd=0 => 0, kd=1 => 1, kd=2 => 2. But note that conv3d with pad=1 and kernel=3 on output size 2 uses output indices 0 and 1. For kd=2 at output index 2, that would correspond to input index i = output - kd + pad = 2 - 2 + 1 = 1. Wait, in conv3d, output[i] = sum_{kd} input[i + kd - pad] * weight[kd]. For i=1 (last output), the valid inputs are i+kd-pad = 1+kd-1 = kd. For kd=0: input[0], kd=1: input[1], kd=2: input[2] which is out of bounds because input size is 2. So in conv3d, the kernel is applied with zero-padding beyond the boundaries. In the transposed conv, the corresponding contributions from input[1] to output[2] are not present because conv3d would have padded zeros at input[2] (since pad=1 means input is padded by 1 on each side, so there is a virtual input[2] = 0). Therefore, in transposed conv, input[1] should NOT contribute to output[2]. Wait, but the adjoint of zero-padding is also zero at the boundaries? Let's think carefully. Standard conv3d with pad=1 on input of size D_out=2: the padded input has size 4 (indices -1,0,1,2). But the output is computed at positions i such that i*stride + kd - pad is in [-1, D_out]?? Actually for stride=1, output index o ranges from 0 to D_out-1 (i.e., 0,1). The formula is: out[o] = sum_{kd} in_padded[o + kd] * weight[kd]. where in_padded has size D_out + 2*pad = 4, with in_padded[p] = in[p - pad] for p in [0, D_out+2*pad-1], with out-of-range treated as zero. So for o=0: in_padded[0+kd] = in[kd - pad] = in[kd-1]. kd=0 => in[-1]=0, kd=1=>in[0], kd=2=>in[1]. For o=1: in_padded[1+kd] = in[kd]?? Actually 1+kd -1 = kd. kd=0=>in[0], kd=1=>in[1], kd=2=>in[2]=0. So the virtual in[2] is zero. In the transposed conv, we are computing the adjoint: for each input element in[i], we add weight[kd] * in[i] to out[i - kd + pad]? Let's derive properly. Let the forward conv be: y = A * x, where x is padded input vector of length D_out+2*pad, y is output vector of length D_in. The matrix A has entries A[o, p] = weight[kd] if p = o + kd + pad? Let's define proper indexing. Let x be original input of length D_out. Let x_pad be padded vector of length D_out + 2*pad, where x_pad[p] = x[p - pad] if 0 <= p - pad < D_out, else 0. Then conv output y[o] = sum_{kd=0}^{Kd-1} x_pad[o + kd] * w[kd] for o = 0..D_in-1, where D_in = floor((D_out + 2*pad - Kd)/stride) + 1. With stride=1, D_in = D_out + 2*pad - Kd + 1. The transposed conv is: given y (length D_in), compute x (length D_out) such that x = A^T y, where A is the matrix representing the conv operation from x_pad to y. But x_pad is just injection of x into a larger vector with zeros outside. A^T maps y to a vector of length D_out+2*pad, then we need to extract the central part? Wait, no. In PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d uses weight shape (C_in, C_out//groups, K, K) and the operation is equivalent to the gradient w.r.t input of conv2d with weight shape (C_out, C_in//groups, K, K). Let's recall the relationship between conv2d and conv_transpose2d weight. If conv2d weight is W of shape (C_out, C_in//groups, K, K), then the transpose operation (adjoint) applied to input y of shape (N, C_out, H_out, W_out) to produce x of shape (N, C_in, H_in, W_in) uses weight W' of shape (C_in, C_out//groups, K, K). In PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d's weight is exactly this W'. But what is the relationship between W and W'? They are the same shape but transposed in channel dimensions: W'[c_in, c_out_local, ...] = W[c_out, c_in_local, ...]. Actually in the gradient computation for conv2d w.r.t input, you convolve the output gradient with the weight flipped spatially (and swapped channel dimensions). So W' is the spatially flipped weight of W, with channel dimensions swapped. Wait, is it spatially flipped? Let's consider conv2d: forward uses weight w. The gradient w.r.t input is computed by convolving the grad_output with w_rot180 (i.e., flipped both horizontally and vertically). In PyTorch, the ConvTranspose2d layer is designed to be the adjoint: if you call conv_transpose with weight = w_rot180_swapped, you get the adjoint. But the user supplies the weight directly. So in our custom kernel, we are given the weight of ConvTranspose3d, which is already W' (the appropriate weight for the transposed operation). Therefore, to compute the transposed conv, we can simply use the direct accumulation approach with the given weight as-is (no flipping). That is: for each input element, add weight * input to output patch, with offsets determined by stride and padding as described. This is the standard inverse im2col. But we need to ensure the padding handling matches exactly. In direct accumulation, for each input element at (d_in, h_in, w_in), we write to output positions (d_in*stride_d + kd - pad_d, ...). However, is the pad_d subtracted or added? Let's look at typical transposed convolution implementations. In PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d, the relationship between conv2d and conv_transpose2d is: Given a conv2d with weight W, stride S, padding P, output_padding OP. The transposed conv's output size is: H_out = (H_in - 1)*S - 2*P + K + OP. And the transposed conv is equivalent to: 1. Upsample the input by inserting S-1 zeros between elements. 2. Pad the upsampled input with (K - P - 1) on each side. 3. Convolve with weight rotated 180 degrees? Actually the weight is stored as the transposed conv weight, so if we want to emulate conv_transpose using standard conv, we would do: conv_transpose(input, weight) = conv(upsampled_input_padded, weight_flipped)? Let's derive. Standard conv2d with stride S, padding P, kernel K: Input I of size H_in, output O of size H_out = floor((H_in + 2P - K)/S) + 1. Transposed conv with same S, P, K, output_padding OP aims to produce an output O' of size H_out' = (H_out - 1)*S - 2P + K + OP. If we consider the "adjoint" operation, each output pixel O[o] receives contributions from input pixels I[i] where i is in [o*S - P, o*S - P + K - 1]. So in the forward direction, O[o] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} I[o*S - P + k] * W[k] (with zero padding for out-of-range I). Therefore, the adjoint operation (transposed conv) maps gradient g[o] to input gradient grad_I[i] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} g[o'] * W[k] where i = o'*S - P + k. Rearranging: for each o', we add W[k] * g[o'] to grad_I[o'*S - P + k]. But note that o' is the input to the transposed conv (let's call it x), and grad_I is the output of the transposed conv (y). So the direct accumulation formula is: y[i] += sum_{o'} x[o'] * W[k] where i = o'*S - P + k. Which can be rewritten as: for each o' (input index), for each k: i = o'*S - P + k. If i is within output bounds, y[i] += x[o'] * W[k]. Alternatively, in multi-d: for each input spatial position (d_in, h_in, w_in), and each kernel offset (kd, kh, kw): d_out = d_in * stride_d + kd - pad_d h_out = h_in * stride_h + kh - pad_h w_out = w_in * stride_w + kw - pad_w if valid, accumulate x * weight[kd, kh, kw]. Note: There is no flip of weight here. This formula is correct for the adjoint of a conv with stride, pad, and the SAME weight orientation (not flipped). But wait, the adjoint of a conv uses the same weight but flipped spatially? Let's verify with 1D. Let conv: y[n] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[n - k + P] * w[k] (with appropriate padding). Actually the standard conv formula with stride=1, pad=P is: y[i] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[i + k - P] * w[k] (where x is zero outside bounds). This is the same as earlier: y[i] = sum_{k} x_pad[i + k] * w[k]. Let's write x_pad[p] = x[p - P] if valid else 0. Then y[i] = sum_{k} x_pad[i + k] * w[k]. Matrix A: y = A x_pad, where A[i, p] = w[k] if p = i + k, else 0. So A[i, p] = w[p - i] if 0 <= p - i < K, else 0. Then A^T[p, i] = A[i, p] = w[p - i] if 0 <= p - i < K, else 0. So the transposed operation mapping y_grad to x_pad_grad is: x_pad_grad[p] = sum_i A^T[p, i] * y_grad[i] = sum_i w[p - i] * y_grad[i] for i such that 0 <= p - i < K. Equivalently, for each i (input index to transposed conv, which is y_grad[i]), we add w[k] * y_grad[i] to x_pad_grad[p] where p = i + k. Then x_grad[p - P] = x_pad_grad[p] (the original unpadded input gradient). So the output of transposed conv (x_grad) at index j = p - P gets contributions from y_grad[i] with w[k] where j = i + k - P. That is exactly: j = i * 1 + k - P. So the formula is: for each input index i, for each k, output[j] += input[i] * w[k] where j = i + k - P. There's no flip of w. But wait, PyTorch's ConvTranspose weight is W', not W. However, since W' is already the weight for this operation, using it directly in this accumulation yields the correct result. The user passes W' to our custom kernel (by copying the ConvTranspose3d weight). So indeed, direct accumulation with weight as-is, no flipping, and j = i*stride + k - pad, is correct. Let's test with our earlier example: D_in=2, stride=1, pad=1, K=3. Input x[0], x[1]. Weight w[0], w[1], w[2]. Output y[j] initially zeros. For i=0: k=0 => j=-1 (skip), k=1 => j=0 => y[0] += x[0]*w[1]; k=2 => j=1 => y[1] += x[0]*w[2]. For i=1: k=0 => j=0 => y[0] += x[1]*w[0]; k=1 => j=1 => y[1] += x[1]*w[1]; k=2 => j=2 (skip because D_out=2). So y[0] = x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0]; y[1] = x[0]*w[2] + x[1]*w[1]. Now consider conv3d forward with stride=1, pad=1, K=3 on an input of size D_out=2 to produce output of size D_in=2. Let input to conv be y (the same y we computed). Conv output o[i] = sum_k y[i + k - 1] * w[k] (with y[-1]=y[2]=0). o[0] = y[-1]*w[0] + y[0]*w[1] + y[1]*w[2] = 0 + (x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0])*w[1] + (x[0]*w[2] + x[1]*w[1])*w[2] = x[0]*(w[1]^2 + w[2]^2) + x[1]*(w[0]*w[1] + w[1]*w[2]). That's not equal to x[0] or x[1] generally. Wait, the adjoint property is A * A^T = I only if A is orthogonal, which is not true. The transposed conv is not the inverse; it's the adjoint with respect to the inner product. It's used in backpropagation: if forward is y = A x, then grad w.r.t x is A^T grad_y. So forward conv applied to output of transposed conv does NOT recover the original input, unless specific conditions. But the question is about matching PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d exactly, not about being an inverse. So direct accumulation with weight as given is the right algorithmic approach. Now we need to think about performance. The straightforward naive kernel (one thread per output element) may be too slow. But maybe for the given sizes, it's okay? Let's examine the sizes: batch_size = 8 in_channels = 32 out_channels = 32 kernel_size = (3,5,7) depth = 12 height = 24 width = 48 stride = (2,2,2) padding = (1,2,3) output_padding = (1,1,1) groups = 4 Compute output sizes: D_out = (12 - 1)*2 - 2*1 + (3 - 1) + 1 + 1 = 11*2 - 2 + 2 + 1 + 1 = 22 - 2 + 2 + 2 = 24? Let's compute carefully: (12-1)*2 = 22. -2*1 = -2 => 20. + (3-1)=+2 => 22. + output_pad_d=+1 => 23. +1 => 24. So D_out=24. H_out = (24-1)*2 - 2*2 + (5-1) + 1 + 1 = 23*2 -4 +4 +1 +1 = 46 -4 +4 +2 = 48. Wait: 23*2=46, -4=42, +4=46, +1=47, +1=48. H_out=48. W_out = (48-1)*2 - 2*3 + (7-1) + 1 + 1 = 47*2 -6 +6 +1 +1 = 94 -6 +6 +2 = 96. W_out=96. So output shape: (8, 32, 24, 48, 96). That's a large tensor: 8*32*24*48*96 = 8*32*110592 = 256*110592 = 28,311,552 elements. About 28.3 million floats ~ 113 MB. Input shape: 8*32*12*24*48 = 8*32*13824 = 256*13824 = 3,538,944 elements ~ 14 MB. Weight shape: in_channels=32, out_channels_per_group = 32/4 = 8. Weight shape: (32, 8, 3, 5, 7). Total weight params = 32*8*3*5*7 = 256*105 = 26,880. Small. The naive approach of iterating over all input elements and scattering to output (atomicAdd) would have work = N*C_in*D_in*H_in*W_in * C_out_per_group * Kd*Kh*Kw = 8*32*12*24*48 * 8 * 3*5*7 = 3,538,944 * 8 * 105 = 3,538,944 * 840 = ~2.97 billion operations. That's a lot. Using atomicAdd on global memory for each accumulation would be terrible. Alternatively, we can do a GEMM-based approach: reshape the operation into a matrix multiplication. For transposed convolution, the standard approach is: - For each group, for each sample in batch: - Use col2im: The operation is y = col2im( X * W ), where X is the input unfolded? For standard Conv2d (forward), we do: - im2col: For each output spatial location (oh, ow), extract a column of size (C_in//groups * Kh * Kw) from the input (considering stride, pad). - GEMM: weight (C_out//groups, C_in//groups * Kh * Kw) @ columns (C_in//groups * Kh * Kw, out_h * out_w) = output features (C_out//groups, out_h * out_w). - Reshape to output. Actually, ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, ...) is the transpose of a Conv3d(out_channels, in_channels, ...). Let's check: Conv3d(in, out, ...) maps (N, in, ...) to (N, out, ...). Let's be precise. Per group: - x has shape (C_in_g, D_in, H_in, W_in). Flatten spatial: x_2d shape (C_in_g, D_in*H_in*W_in). - W_t has shape (C_in_g, C_out_g, Kd, Kh, Kw). This value should be accumulated into output at: d_out = d_in * stride_d + kd - pad_d h_out = h_in * stride_h + kh - pad_h w_out = w_in * stride_w + kw - pad_w (if valid). - This col2im is basically summing contributions from all columns into the output tensor. This is the standard approach and is much more efficient because the heavy compute is in GEMM (C_in_g * C_out_g*K^3 * D_in*H_in*W_in) which is the same total number of multiply-adds as the naive approach but is highly optimized using cuBLAS. The col2im step is a scatter-add, but we can implement it as a simple kernel. However, in our case, the weight is small and the spatial dimensions are moderate. Actually, yes! Transposed convolution is the transpose of regular convolution. If regular conv uses weight w[k] at offset k, then transposed conv uses w[k] but with the spatial relationship reversed. Actually, let's think about this more carefully. Regular conv: y[i] = sum_k x[i+k] * w[k] (using valid region) Transposed conv (the adjoint): For the adjoint, if <y, conv(x, w)> = <transpose_conv(y, w), x> LHS = sum_i y[i] * sum_k x[i+k] * w[k] = sum_i sum_k y[i] * x[i+k] * w[k] Let j = i+k, then i = j-k: = sum_j sum_k y[j-k] * x[j] * w[k] = sum_j x[j] * sum_k y[j-k] * w[k] So transpose_conv(y, w)[j] = sum_k y[j-k] * w[k] If we compare with scatter formulation above where input z at position id contributes z[id] * w[kd] to output[id*s + kd - p], then for output position j = id*s + kd - p: z[id] * w[kd] where id = (j + p - kd) / s So transpose_conv(y, w)[j] = sum_{k, (j+p-k)/s valid} y[(j+p-k)/s] * w[k] With dilated input: let dilated_y[j'] = y[(j'-p)/s] when (j'-p) % s == 0 Then transpose_conv(y, w)[j] = sum_k dilated_y[j + k] * w[k] Wait! So the "conv on dilated input" formulation must be wrong about what padding means in ConvTranspose. According to PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose: "The padding argument effectively adds dilation * (kernel_size - 1) - padding amount of zero padding to both sizes of the input." Oh! This is the key. `padding` in ConvTranspose is NOT adding padding to the dilated input. Instead, it's a "negative padding" or cropping. Specifically, the effective padding added is: (K - 1) - p. So for p=1, K=3: effective padding = 3 - 1 - 1 = 1. Which matches what I computed. For p=0, K=3: effective padding = 3 - 1 - 0 = 2. This means: 1. Dilate input with stride 2. Pad with (K-1-p) zeros on both sides 3. Apply regular conv with stride=1, padding=0 4. In any case, for the kernel: - We compute output according to the scatter formula - For output positions that have no valid contributions, the value is 0 (or bias) Now I need to be careful about output_padding. In PyTorch, output_padding adds zeros (or more precisely, extends the output shape, with the extension filled by whatever the conv computes). Does it actually affect which (id, kd) pairs are valid? Actually, yes! Because with output_padding=1, the output size is 6 instead of 5. For od=5, we computed c*w2 above. So it's not just "add zeros at the end". The conv computation is extended. How does this happen in the "effective padding" formulation? effective_padding = K - 1 - p = 3 - 1 - 1 = 1 dilated_size = 5 padded_dilated_size = 5 + 2*1 = 7 conv with K=3, op=1: output = 7 - 3 + 1 + 1 = 6 Wait, how does adding output_padding change the conv? For those extra positions, the conv is computed with the assumption that there's additional padding in the input. So if op=1, it's like the padded dilated input has size 7 + 1 = 8 instead of 7. In the effective-padding formulation, the conv uses weight [w0, w1, w2] directly. In the scatter formulation, the weight is used as-is. But we established earlier that scatter gives the correct result matching backprop. Wait, let me re-examine. In the effective-padding formulation, I said conv output[5] uses [c, 0, 0]. As discussed, weight is (in_channels, out_channels_per_group, *kernel_size). And for output channel oc in group g, the computation is: output[b, oc, ...] = sum_{ic in group g} input[b, ic, ...] convolved with weight[ic, oc_local, ...] Where "convolved with" means the transposed convolution operation. So in the scatter formulation: For each input position (id, ih, iw), each kernel offset (kd, kh, kw), and each output channel oc in the same group: output[b, oc, id*s + kd - p, ih*s + kh - p, iw*s + kw - p] += input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] * weight[ic, oc_local, kd, kh, kw] With the understanding that out-of-bound writes are ignored, and for (kd, kh, kw) where the output position is not an integer or out of bounds, nothing is written. Actually wait, id*s + kd - p must be the output index. But s and p are per-dimension. So: od = id * stride_d + kd - padding_d oh = ih * stride_h + kh - padding_h ow = iw * stride_w + kw - padding_w And od must be in [0, D_out-1], etc. For a reverse formulation (given od, find contributing id and kd): od = id * s + kd - p kd = od + p - id * s Since kd ∈ [0, K-1], we have: 0 ≤ od + p - id*s ≤ K-1 id*s ≤ od + p ≤ id*s + K - 1 id ≤ (od + p) / s id ≥ (od + p - K + 1) / s So for a given od, id can range from ceil((od + p - K + 1) / s) to floor((od + p) / s). And for each valid id, kd = od + p - id*s, which automatically satisfies 0 ≤ kd ≤ K-1. This formulation is very clean! For each output position, we iterate over valid input positions id and compute the corresponding kd. Similarly for ih, iw. Then for each valid (id, ih, iw), we iterate over oc and ic in the group and accumulate. This is very convenient for the kernel. One optimization I can try: use vectorized loads (float4) for both input and weights when possible. Another optimization: since many output pixels share the same input pixels, I can process output pixels in a way that maximizes cache reuse. For example, compute all output channels for a given spatial position before moving to the next spatial position. This keeps the input pixel in registers/cache while applying different weights. Wait, that's an interesting idea. If I process one output spatial position at a time but compute all 32 output channels for it: - For each valid (id, ih, iw, kd, kh, kw): - Load input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] for all ic in the group - For each oc in the group: - output[b, oc, od, oh, ow] += input_value[ic] * weight[ic, oc_local, kd, kh, kw] This way, each input value is loaded once and used for all 8 output channels in the group. That reduces input bandwidth by 8x! And for weights, we load weight[ic, oc_local, kd, kh, kw] for all 8 output channels, which are contiguous in memory. Perfect for vectorized loads. Let me formalize this: For each output spatial position (od, oh, ow) and batch b: For each group g: Initialize accumulators for 8 output channels: acc[8] = {0} For each valid (id, ih, iw, kd, kh, kw): For ic_local in [0, 7]: input_val = input[b, g*8+ic_local, id, ih, iw] weight_vec = load 8 floats: weight[g*8+ic_local, 0:8, kd, kh, kw] For oc_local in [0, 7]: acc[oc_local] += input_val * weight_vec[oc_local] For oc_local in [0, 7]: output[b, g*8+oc_local, od, oh, ow] = acc[oc_local] + bias Number of multiply-adds per output spatial position and group: For each valid spatial combination (at most 24), and each ic_local (8), and each oc_local (8): 24 * 8 * 8 = 1536 multiply-adds. But the number of weight loads is 24 * 8 = 192 vectors of 8 floats each. That's 1536 float loads, or 384 float4 loads. Input loads: 24 * 8 = 192 float loads. But each input float is used for 8 multiplies (one per oc_local), so this is efficient. Total float loads: 192 (input) + 1536 (weight) = 1728 floats = 6.9 KB per output spatial position, per group, per batch. Output writes: 8 floats = 32 bytes. For all 28M output elements, if organized this way, we'd load each input element multiple times but each time use it efficiently. Actually, this approach has a nice property: for each input channel, we load its value once per valid spatial position, and then multiply by 8 weights (for the 8 output channels) before moving to the next input channel or spatial position. This means the loop count varies significantly across the output domain, making warp-level uniformity challenging. Given all this complexity, maybe the simplest approach is actually best: one thread per output element, with the exact iteration count for that element. The hardware can handle moderate divergence, and for most of the output domain, the counts are near the maximum. For the interior regions: - d: 2 for odd, 1 for even - h: 3 for even >= 2, 2 for odd - w: 3 for even >= 2, 4 for odd >= 3 Interior combinations: For even d (1), even h (3), even w (3): 9 combinations For odd d (2), even h (3), even w (3): 18 combinations For even d (1), odd h (2), even w (3): 6 combinations For even d (1), even h (3), odd w (4): 12 combinations ... Average: roughly between 9 and 24, maybe around 15 for most positions. So each thread does about 15 * 8 * 8 = 960 multiply-adds on average. Not too bad. Actually, there's an even better approach. Instead of iterating over valid (id, ih, iw) and deriving kd, kh, kw, we can iterate over id, ih, iw and derive kd, kh, kw. Then we check if kd, kh, kw are in [0, K-1]. For a given od: id_min = max(0, ceil((od + p - K + 1) / s)) id_max = min(D-1, floor((od + p) / s)) For each id in [id_min, id_max]: kd = od + p - id * s if 0 <= kd < K: valid! This gives the same result and might be simpler to compute in the kernel. Similarly for h and w. For our test case, since the range is small, we can just iterate id from 0 to D-1 and check the condition. But that would be 12 iterations for d instead of ~2. However, if we unroll or use the exact range, it's fine. Wait, actually computing id_min and id_max once per thread, then iterating over that small range, is quite efficient. OK, I think I'm overthinking this. Let me just write a reasonable kernel and test it. I'll start with a simple output-per-thread kernel with block=(256) and exploit the fact that we skip zero multiplications. Let me draft the CUDA kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int depth, int height, int width, int out_depth, int out_height, int out_width, int kernel_d, int kernel_h, int kernel_w, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int padding_d, int padding_h, int padding_w, int output_padding_d, int output_padding_h, int output_padding_w, int groups ) { int out_ch_per_group = out_channels / groups; int in_ch_per_group = in_channels / groups; int total_out = batch_size * out_channels * out_depth * out_height * out_width; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_out) return; // Decode idx to (b, oc, od, oh, ow) int tmp = idx; int ow = tmp % out_width; tmp /= out_width; int oh = tmp % out_height; tmp /= out_height; int od = tmp % out_depth; tmp /= out_depth; int oc = tmp % out_channels; tmp /= out_channels; int b = tmp; int g = oc / out_ch_per_group; int oc_local = oc % out_ch_per_group; float acc = 0.0f; // For each valid input depth int id_min = max(0, (od + padding_d - kernel_d + 1 + stride_d - 1) / stride_d); int id_max = min(depth - 1, (od + padding_d) / stride_d); for (int id = id_min; id <= id_max; ++id) { int kd = od + padding_d - id * stride_d; if (kd < 0 || kd >= kernel_d) continue; for (int ih = max(0, (oh + padding_h - kernel_h + 1 + stride_h - 1) / stride_h); ih <= min(height - 1, (oh + padding_h) / stride_h); ++ih) { int kh = oh + padding_h - ih * stride_h; if (kh < 0 || kh >= kernel_h) continue; for (int iw = max(0, (ow + padding_w - kernel_w + 1 + stride_w - 1) / stride_w); iw <= min(width - 1, (ow + padding_w) / stride_w); ++iw) { int kw = ow + padding_w - iw * stride_w; if (kw < 0 || kw >= kernel_w) continue; for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < in_ch_per_group; ++ic_local) { int ic = g * in_ch_per_group + ic_local; float in_val = input[((b * in_channels + ic) * depth + id) * height + ih) * width + iw]; float w_val = weight[(((ic * out_ch_per_group + oc_local) * kernel_d + kd) * kernel_h + kh) * kernel_w + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) { acc += bias[oc]; } output[((b * out_channels + oc) * out_depth + od) * out_height + oh) * out_width + ow] = acc; } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about integer division rounding. The formula `ceil(a/b)` for positive integers is `(a + b - 1) / b`. In my code: id_min = max(0, ceil((od + padding_d - kernel_d + 1) / stride_d)) id_max = min(depth - 1, floor((od + padding_d) / stride_d)) For positive values, floor division is just integer division `/` in C++ for positive numbers. Ceil division is `(numerator + denominator - 1) / denominator` for positive numerator. But `od + padding_d - kernel_d + 1` can be negative! If it's negative, ceil(negative / positive) might be negative, and we clip to 0 anyway. So: id_min = max(0, (od + padding_d - kernel_d + 1 + stride_d - 1) / stride_d) = max(0, (od + padding_d - kernel_d + stride_d) / stride_d) Let me verify with the example: od=0, p=1, K=3, s=2: id_min = max(0, (0 + 1 - 3 + 2) / 2) = max(0, 0 / 2) = 0 ✓ od=0 requires id=0, kd=1. id_max = min(D-1, (0 + 1) / 2) = min(2, 0) = 0 So id=0 is the only value. kd = 0 + 1 - 0*2 = 1. ✓ od=1: id_min = max(0, (1 + 1 - 3 + 2) / 2) = max(0, 1 / 2) = 0 id_max = min(2, (1+1)/2) = min(2, 1) = 1 id=0: kd = 1+1-0 = 2 ✓ id=1: kd = 1+1-2 = 0 ✓ od=2: id_min = max(0, (2+1-3+2)/2) = max(0, 2/2) = 1 id_max = min(2, (2+1)/2) = min(2, 1) = 1 id=1: kd = 2+1-2 = 1 ✓ Good. But for od=5 with op=1: id_min = max(0, (5+1-3+2)/2) = max(0, 5/2) = 2 id_max = min(2, (5+1)/2) = min(2, 3) = 2 id=2: kd = 5+1-4 = 2 ✓ output[5] = c*w2. Now, what if id_max computes to a value >= depth and id_min also >= depth? Then the loop doesn't execute, and output is 0. That corresponds to output positions where there are no valid contributions. Wait, but I need to be careful about the output_padding region. For the test, D_out=24 with D_in=12. od=23: id_min = max(0, (23+1-3+2)/2) = max(0, 23/2) = 11 id_max = min(11, (23+1)/2) = min(11, 12) = 11 id=11: kd = 23+1-22 = 2 So there IS a valid input position for od=23. Good. For the "extra" output_padding positions, are they all zero? od=23 is actually the last output position (since D_out=24, indices 0-23). And it has a valid input. So output_padding doesn't create all-zero positions in this case. OK, the kernel formula looks correct. Now, about performance. The kernel as written has the loops over id, ih, iw in the inner loops, and ic in the innermost loop. This means: For each valid spatial combination (id, ih, iw, kd, kh, kw): For each ic_local: Load input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] Load weight[ic, oc_local, kd, kh, kw] Multiply and accumulate With 256 threads per block, if all threads are computing the same oc but different spatial positions, the weight loads for the same (ic, oc_local, kd, kh, kw) differ between threads. That's not ideal. If all threads compute different oc but the same spatial position, the input loads are the same (broadcast), and weight loads for the same (ic, kd, kh, kw) but different oc_local are contiguous. That's better. But as discussed, having different oc in the same warp is problematic because they might be in different groups. What if we restructure the kernel to process one group at a time, and within a group, have contiguous threads handle contiguous spatial positions and contiguous output channels? Let me try: each block handles 1 batch, 1 group (8 in-channels, 8 out-channels), and some number of spatial positions. Block = (8, 32) → 256 threads. - x: 8 output channels (all channels in the group) - y: 32 spatial positions All threads in the block are in the same group. Grid: - x: not used (or 1) - y: not used (or 1) - z: spatial blocks Actually, let me change the grid layout: Grid = (spatial_blocks, groups * batch_size) Block = (8, 32) Each block computes 8 output channels for 32 spatial positions, for 1 group and 1 batch. For spatial decomposition: total spatial positions = 24*48*96 = 110,592. With 32 positions per block: 110,592 / 32 = 3456 spatial blocks. Total blocks: 3456 * (4 * 8) = 3456 * 32 = 110,592 blocks. Each thread computes 1 output element. Within a warp (32 threads): 8 channels for 4 spatial positions. For warp 0: spatial positions 0, 1, 2, 3 (in some ordering). For each valid spatial combination, 8 threads load contiguous weights for the 8 channels. And 4 groups of 8 threads (one per spatial position) load input from different spatial positions. The input loads might not be coalesced, but they can be cached. The weight loads are coalesced within each sub-group of 8 threads. This might be good enough. Let me also think about how to improve input cache hits. If the 4 spatial positions in a warp are consecutive in some dimension where input overlaps, we get better cache reuse. For example, if consecutive spatial positions differ in ow by 1, they share many iw values. So processing spatial positions in row-major order (ow fastest) is good. Let me assign the 32 spatial positions in a block as a 1D tile in row-major order. For example, the block handles a region like: (od_start, oh_start, ow_start) to (od_start, oh_start, ow_start+31) (wrapping as needed). Or a 2x2x8 tile, etc. A 1x1x32 tile (varying ow) would maximize w-dimension overlap. Actually, let me think about what happens when we process 32 consecutive ow values with the same od and oh. For ow=0 to 31: The valid iw ranges overlap significantly. iw typically ranges from 0 to about 16 for these ow values. But different threads in the warp are processing different ow values, so their iw loops have different bounds and iterations. This causes divergence. If I use a tile of 2x4x4 for example: od∈{0,1}, oh∈{0,1,2,3}, ow∈{0,1,2,3} For d even (0): 1 id. For d odd (1): 2 ids. So there's some divergence. For h: oh=0→2, oh=1→2, oh=2→3, oh=3→2. For w: ow=0→2, ow=1→3, ow=2→3, ow=3→4. The loop counts vary, but not wildly. And nearby positions share some input values. I think for the first version, I'll just use a simple 1D spatial decomposition where each block gets a contiguous chunk of spatial positions. The exact details might not matter too much if the kernel is memory/compute efficient. Actually, there's another approach that might be even simpler and more efficient: unroll the spatial combinations explicitly. Since the kernel sizes are small (3, 5, 7) and the number of valid combinations is small (at most 24), we can precompute which (kd, kh, kw) are valid for each (od, oh, ow) phase. But this would require a lot of precomputation. For a simpler first attempt, I'll stick with the loop-over-id approach. One more optimization: I should handle the loop ordering to maximize reuse. The innermost loop should be over ic_local since the input and weights for consecutive ic_local are memory-adjacent or at least have predictable strides. Wait, input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] for consecutive ic are separated by depth*height*width. That's not contiguous. But with groups=4 and in_ch_per_group=8, we only iterate over 8 channels, which is a small range. Weight[ic, oc_local, kd, kh, kw] for consecutive ic are separated by out_ch_per_group * kd * kh * kw = 8 * 3 * 5 * 7 = 840 floats. Neither is particularly contiguous. But if we load weight for a fixed ic and all oc_local (8 values), that's a contiguous vector of 8 floats. Good for float4 or float2 loads. Similarly, if we load input for a fixed (id, ih, iw) and all ic_local (8 values), that's 8 floats separated by D*H*W. Not contiguous at all. So it makes sense to have the oc_local loop inside the ic_local loop, so that for each ic_local, we load the input once and then use it for all oc_local? No, that would mean loading 8 weights per input value, but the weights are contiguous. Actually, let me think about this differently. For a fixed spatial combination: For ic_local = 0: Load input_0 Load weight_0[0:7] (8 floats, contiguous) For oc_local = 0..7: acc[oc_local] += input_0 * weight_0[oc_local] For ic_local = 1: Load input_1 Load weight_1[0:7] For oc_local = 0..7: acc[oc_local] += input_1 * weight_1[oc_local] This processes one ic_local at a time, loading one input value and one weight vector of 8. The weight vector is contiguous and can be loaded with two float4 instructions. If a thread computes all 8 output channels, it needs 8 accumulators and does 8 multiply-adds per ic_local. That's 8*8 = 64 operations per spatial combination. If a thread computes only 1 output channel, it loads 1 weight per ic_local, but the weights for different threads (different oc_local) are contiguous. A warp of 8 threads computing 8 channels could load all 8 weights with two float4 loads and broadcast the input value. The second approach (one thread per channel, cooperative loading) might have better register usage but requires warp-level cooperation. For simplicity, let me have each thread compute all 8 channels for its spatial position. This requires 8 accumulators (can fit in registers) and loads an 8-float weight vector per ic_local per spatial combination. With at most 24 spatial combinations and 8 ic_local values, each thread loads 24*8 = 192 weight vectors of 8 floats. That's 1536 floats per thread. In 32 registers (512 floats), we can't store all weights, so they must be loaded from global memory. But if the weights fit in L1 cache or are read repeatedly by many threads, the effective bandwidth is lower. Actually, all threads in the block that are in the same group load the same weight values (since they all compute channels in the same group, just different spatial positions). So with 256 threads per block and 32 spatial positions, each weight is loaded 32 times (once per spatial position). With L1 cache of 128 KB per SM on H100, the weights for one group are 27 KB. This easily fits in L1 cache. So after the first few reads, weights are served from L1 cache at very high bandwidth. Similarly, input values for different spatial positions overlap, so L1 cache helps there too. So the memory bandwidth should not be a bottleneck if L1 caching works well. OK, I think the kernel design is reasonable. Let me write a concrete CUDA kernel and test it. One more thought: I can also try using shared memory for weights. If I load all weights for the group (27 KB) into shared memory once per block, all threads can access them at shared memory speed. This avoids L1 cache misses and contention. For 256 threads, loading 27 KB of weights into shared memory is quite fast. And 27 KB fits in the 227 KB limit. With shared memory for weights: 1. At the start of the block, all threads cooperatively load weight[g*8:(g+1)*8, 0:8, 0:3, 0:5, 0:7] into shared memory. 2. Each thread then accesses weights from shared memory. This should be faster than loading from global memory, especially since the weight accesses are random (different kd, kh, kw for different threads). Similarly, I could also load input into shared memory. But since different spatial positions need different input regions, and the total input needed for 32 spatial positions might be large, this is trickier. Let me estimate: for 32 spatial positions in a tile, if they're all with the same od and oh but different ow (e.g., ow=0 to 31), the iw range is roughly 0 to 17 (since ow=31→iw up to floor((31+3)/2)=17). And ih is roughly 0 to 13 or so (depending on oh). So input for one batch, one group (8 channels), id range (say 0-1 or similar), ih range (~14 positions), iw range (~18 positions): 8 * 2 * 14 * 18 ≈ 4032 floats ≈ 16 KB. This also fits in shared memory! So we could load both weights (27 KB) and relevant input (16 KB) into shared memory, totaling 43 KB per block. Still well within limits. But the exact input region depends on the spatial tile. And loading input into shared memory requires knowing which (id, ih, iw) are needed and having all threads participate. Actually, this is getting complex. For the first iteration, let me just write a kernel with shared memory for weights, and see if it compiles and passes correctness. Then I can optimize further. If stride > 1, the input positions that map to a given output position follow this pattern: out = (in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size - 1 - actual_padding + output_padding Hmm, let me think about this differently. ConvTranspose3d effectively computes for each output position (od, oh, ow): acc = sum over all input positions that map here For transposed conv with stride s, the mapping from output to input is: id = (od - kd) / s + padding But only when (od - kd) is divisible by s. Actually, in the forward direction of ConvTranspose3d: - Each element at (b, ic, id, ih, iw) contributes to multiple output positions - The contribution is: input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] * weight[ic, oc/g, kd, kh, kw] added to output[b, oc, od, oh, ow] where od = id * stride_d - padding_d + kd oh = ih * stride_h - padding_h + kh ow = iw * stride_w - padding_w + kw Wait, I think the formula is more like: od = id * stride_d - padding_d + kd But ConvTranspose3d applies padding by effectively cropping the output. For ConvTranspose3d: output[b, oc, od, oh, ow] = sum over ic, kd, kh, kw: input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] * weight[ic, oc/g, kd, kh, kw] where id = (od + 2*padding_d - kd) / stride_d ih = (oh + 2*padding_h - kh) / stride_h iw = (ow + 2*padding_w - kw) / stride_w Hmm wait, that's not right either since PyTorch ConvTranspose3d uses `padding` differently. In ConvTranspose3d, `padding` acts like cropping from the output, not adding zeros to the input. Actually, from the PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose3d: Hout = (Hin - 1) * stride[0] - 2 * padding[0] + dilation[0] * (kernel_size[0] - 1) + output_padding[0] + 1 And the actual relationship is that transposed conv is the gradient of conv. If we have a conv with stride s, padding p, then the transpose is: out_pos = in_pos * s + kernel_offset - p Wait, for the "direct" computation of transposed conv: For each input element at (id, ih, iw), it contributes to output positions: od = id * stride_d - padding_d + kd oh = ih * stride_h - padding_h + kh ow = iw * stride_w - padding_w + kw where kd, kh, kw range from 0 to kernel_size-1. But padding in ConvTranspose3d crops the output, so the valid od range is: od >= 0 od < out_depth So for a given (od, oh, ow), which (id, ih, iw, kd, kh, kw) contribute? The key insight is that for strided convolutions, we can use the "dilated convolution" viewpoint. ConvTranspose3d with stride S is equivalent to: 1. Insert S-1 zeros between input elements in each spatial dimension 2. Perform a regular convolution with padding d*S-S where d is dilation = 1 But actually the more practical approach is to use im2col / GEMM. Let's think about the structure: - For ConvTranspose3d, the forward pass can be seen as: - For each output position and output channel, we need a dot product between the input at certain positions and the filter Actually, a much faster approach is to realize that ConvTranspose3d can be implemented by: 1. For each input position, compute the partial contribution to output using the weight kernel 2. Accumulate these contributions This is like the reverse of pooling. Each input element contributes to `out_channels * kernel_d * kernel_h * kernel_w` output elements. Let me think about this differently. Instead of each thread computing one output element (which requires reading many input elements and weight elements over a potentially large receptive field), each thread could compute the contribution of one input element to its output neighborhood. For strided ConvTranspose3d: - Each input position (id, ih, iw) contributes to output positions where: - od = id * stride_d - padding_d + kd for kd in [0, kernel_d) - oh = ih * stride_h - padding_h + kh for kh in [0, kernel_h) - ow = iw * stride_w - padding_w + kw for kw in [0, kernel_w) But only if od, oh, ow are within [0, out_d/h/w). This approach is more parallelizable because each thread independently computes contributions without synchronization (since we need to accumulate into output). In terms of implementation, one common approach is: 1. For each input element, take its value, multiply by the entire kernel, and add to the corresponding output region 2. Where "corresponding output region" depends on the stride So for each input position, we do a rank-1 update to a kernel-sized region of output. With this formulation, each thread processes one input element and updates the output. But this requires atomic adds. For fp32, we can use atomicAdd. This is a lot but might be manageable if we organize threads well. Actually wait, the atomic adds to different output positions are mostly non-conflicting if different threads write to different output positions. This would have conflicts at the spatial positions! Actually, no - if each thread writes to different channels at the same spatial positions, and if we store channels in separate addresses, there's no conflict. Let me verify output layout: [batch, channels, depth, height, width]. So for fixed (b, spatial), different channels are contiguous? No, in PyTorch's default NCHW layout: - output[b][oc][od][oh][ow] - Channels are the outer dimension. So if thread T1 writes to channel 0 and thread T2 writes to channel 1 at the same spatial position: these are at addresses separated by out_depth * out_height * out_width = 24 * 48 * 96 = 110,592 elements. So no bank conflicts for atomics. But there IS a conflict if two different input elements write to the same output position and channel. For stride=2 and the given padding, different input elements can map to the same output position. Actually, with ConvTranspose3d, two different inputs CAN map to the same output position. That's why we need accumulation. For groups > 1 and channels within groups, can two inputs map to the same output? Yes, if they come from the same group and have different input channels. So for our kernel with input-scatter formulation, we need atomicAdd. Let me look at how cuDNN implements this - it's likely using reduction trees or allowing threads to accumulate in shared memory first. Actually, there's another elegant approach. We can reformulate the transposed convolution as: 1. For each batch and group, reshape the input from [in_ch_per_group, depth, height, width] to [kernel_d*kernel_h*kernel_w, in_ch_per_group, depth*height*width / something] 2. Perform GEMM with weight matrix [out_ch_per_group, in_ch_per_group * kernel_d * kernel_h * kernel_w] 3. Reshape and add for stride This is the standard "col2im" approach used in regular convolutions but adapted for transposed conv. For transposed convolution, the GEMM approach is: - Weight is [in_ch, out_ch_per_group, kd, kh, kw] - Reshape weight to W_mat: [out_ch_per_group, in_ch_per_group * kd * kh * kw] - For each input position: take a patch of the input (just 1 element actually, but conceptually...) - Actually for transposed conv with stride: - The input can be expanded by inserting zeros - Then a regular conv is applied But the zero-insertion is wasteful. Actually, PyTorch/cuDNN likely uses a very optimized specialized kernel. To be faster, we might need to: 1. Use WGMMA (warp group matrix multiply accumulate) on H100 2. Or find a clever decomposition Given the constraints, let me try yet another approach. Instead of computing output elements directly, I'll use shared memory to accumulate results from tiles of input. Here's the idea: tile the output space. Each block processes a tile of output positions. For each block: 1. For each group's output channels, initialize shared memory accumulator for the tile 2. Iterate over input positions that contribute to this tile 3. For each input position, compute which output positions in the tile it contributes to 4. Accumulate in shared memory 5. Write to global memory This avoids global atomics by using shared memory accumulation within a block. But we need to ensure that all input elements contributing to a given output tile are processed by the same block. For a tile of output positions [od_start:od_end, oh_start:oh_end, ow_start:ow_end], the contributing input positions are: - id: (od + padding_d - kd) / stride_d for od in tile, kd in kernel - These id values span a range that depends on tile_size, stride, kernel For tile size T and stride S, the input span is roughly (T + kernel - 1 + 2*padding) / S. Let me think about the linear algebra. A strided convolution can be represented as a matrix C where rows correspond to output positions and columns to input positions. A transposed convolution uses the transpose matrix C^T. An "upsample then conv" also defines some linear transformation, but it's generally NOT the same as C^T. For the specific case where the upsampling is by zero-insertion and the conv is regular, the matrix is actually C'*U where U is the upsampling matrix. This is NOT equal to C^T in general. So we can't just implement ConvTranspose as "upsample + conv". But wait, for specific configurations, there are equivalences. And PyTorch internally might use this or other optimizations. Some are the same. For those reading the same iw, cache handles it. For those reading adjacent iw, the reads are coalesced. Actually, I think the key insight is that for this problem, we should use GEMM via im2col. Let me look at how to implement transposed convolution using GEMM. For regular convolution, im2col converts the input image to columns where each column is a flattened patch. Then we do GEMM: output = weight_matrix * im2col(input). For transposed convolution, we want the transpose operation: output = col2im(weight_matrix^T * input_as_columns). Specifically: - Input has shape [batch, in_ch, D, H, W] - We reshape it to [batch, in_ch, D*H*W] then to [batch * D * H * W, in_ch] (but need to think about grouping) Actually, for transposed conv, we can think of it as: 1. Flatten input spatial: [batch * D_in * H_in * W_in, in_ch_per_group] 2. Multiply by weight reshape: [in_ch_per_group, out_ch_per_group * Kd * Kh * Kw] 3. This gives: [batch * D_in * H_in * W_in, out_ch_per_group * Kd * Kh * Kw] 4. Then use col2im to scatter-add these into the output The col2im step accounts for the stride by placing each column element at the correct output position. This is exactly what optimized libraries do internally. And we can use PyTorch's built-in tensor operations or cuBLAS for the GEMM part. Actually, PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d already does this internally, calling cuDNN which is very optimized. So doing the same thing in Python with PyTorch ops won't be faster. Unless... we can fuse some operations, use a better algorithm, or avoid overheads. Hmm, another thought. Actually, I think I recall now: `output_padding` in transposed convolution is NOT padding with zeros. Instead, the transposed convolution is defined as the transpose of a regular conv whose output size is reduced by output_padding. So if a regular conv with padding P maps N_in to N_out, the transposed conv maps N_out back to N_in. But if we want the transposed conv to map to a SLIGHTLY LARGER size than N_in, we add output_padding. So output_padding just adjusts the output size formula. The actual computation still uses the full transposed convolution. For od=4 in the 1D example: it DOES get contributions. The transposed conv is NOT padding zeros at the end. I was wrong about output_padding! It's not empty padding; it's just a size adjustment that allows the output to be larger. So my kernel's handling of output_padding is actually correct: we just compute the output size with output_padding, and then compute the transposed conv normally for all those positions. In PyTorch, `ConvTranspose2d`/`ConvTranspose3d` store the weight internally and then transpose it during the forward pass. Specifically, `ConvTranspose2d` stores transposed weights: the `self.weight` has shape [in_channels, out_channels/groups, H, W], but conceptually, it represents the transpose of the conv weight. For ConvTranspose3d, if it implements the transpose of a strided conv, does it flip the weight or not? It's the transpose of: `y[i] = sum_k x[(i - k)*S + ...] * flipped_weight[k]`. The transpose would use the same (flipped) weight. Actually, the common mathematical definition says: transposed convolution with stride S and kernel K is exactly the transpose of convolution with stride S and kernel K. Since convolution internally flips the kernel, the transposed operation uses the flipped kernel too. In practice, many deep learning frameworks (including PyTorch) define ConvTranspose3d so that the stored weight is used WITHOUT additional flipping. The "flipping" is part of the transposed algorithm's indexing. I think my formula is correct, but let me try one more thing: maybe PyTorch's weight for ConvTranspose3d is conceptually the transpose, and my direct indexing should actually use `weight[oc_local][ic][kd][kh][kw]` instead? No, that can't be right because the weight shape is [in_ch, out_ch_per_group, *kernel]. Actually, let me think about this from first principles. The Conv operation (with stride=1 for simplicity) can be written as: `y[c_out][i] = sum_{c_in} sum_k x[c_in][i + k] * w[c_out][c_in][K-1-k]` Or with padding: `y[c_out][i] = sum_{c_in} sum_k x[c_in][i + pad + k] * w[c_out][c_in][K-1-k]` Wait, for conv WITH padding, the indexing is: `y[i] = sum_k x[i + k - pad] * w[K-1-k]` For transposed conv, if it's the transpose of the above: For each x[j], it contributes to y[i] where i appears in the sum for j. In the conv: y[i] gets contribution from x[j=i+k-pad] with weight w[K-1-k]. So x[j] contributes to y[i=j-k+pad] with weight w[K-1-k] = w[k'] where k' = K-1-k. Or equivalently, x[j] contributes to y[i] where k = j - i + pad, with weight w[K-1 - (j-i+pad)]. This is getting messy. Let me use the simpler "scatter" view: In transposed conv, each input x[j] is multiplied by the kernel and placed at output positions that correspond to where x[j] was read from in the forward conv. For Conv with kernel K, stride S, pad P: To compute y[i], we read x at positions: id = i*S - P + k for k in [0, K). So x[j] appears in y[i] for all i where j = i*S - P + k for some k in [0, K). => i = (j + P - k) / S. And the weight used is w[k] (from the regular conv). In PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d, does it use w[k] or w[K-1-k]? If ConvTranspose is the EXACT transpose of Conv, and Conv uses w[K-1-k], then ConvTranspose should also use w[K-1-k]. But PyTorch might have designed ConvTranspose3d to use w[k] directly (no flip) to match the common expectation that transposed conv "upsamples and applies a regular conv." Actually, in many deep learning frameworks, `ConvTranspose2d` is defined as the gradient of `Conv2d`, which means it MUST use the same weight (without flip) because the gradient of Conv w.r.t. input is a transposed convolution with the same weight matrix. In PyTorch, `Conv2d` computes cross-correlation, NOT convolution. That is, it does NOT flip the kernel. From the docs: "cross-correlation a 2D input with a 2D kernel" So `Conv2d` in PyTorch is actually cross-correlation: `y[i] = sum_k x[i + k - pad] * w[k]` Then the transpose of this operation is: `x[j] contributes to y[i] where j = i*S - pad + k, with weight w[k]` So `i = (j + pad - k) / S` And the weight used in the transposed conv is `w[k]`, the SAME as in the forward pass (since forward didn't flip). But this is complex. Let me first get correctness working, then optimize. Simplest organization: - Each thread handles all output channels for ONE output spatial position. - Thread ID = spatial position index (linearized over batch, od, oh, ow). - Grid covers all spatial positions. - Each thread loops over channels and kernel positions, accumulating in local registers, then writes all output channels. Output writes: thread i writes to spatial position i for ALL channels. Since channels are not contiguous in memory, this requires scattered writes. But each thread only does 32 writes (for 32 channels), so it's not too bad. Input reads: thread i reads input based on its spatial position. Adjacent threads read adjacent-ish input positions along W dimension, which is the fastest-changing dimension. This should be somewhat coalesced. Weight reads: all threads read the same weights for overlapping spatial positions, but different threads at different spatial positions might read different weights. However, weights are small (26.9 KB), so they should fit in L1 cache and be reused. This should work reasonably well. Let me write it.

callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = '''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\n__global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels,\n int depth, int height, int width,\n int out_depth, int out_height, int out_width,\n int kernel_d, int kernel_h, int kernel_w,\n int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w,\n int padding_d, int padding_h, int padding_w,\n int groups\n) {\n int out_ch_per_group = out_channels / groups;\n int in_ch_per_group = in_channels / groups;\n \n // Total number of output spatial positions (across batch and all spatial dims)\n int total_spatial = batch_size * out_depth * out_height * out_width;\n \n int spatial_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n \n if (spatial_idx >= total_spatial) return;\n \n // Decode spatial_idx to (b, od, oh, ow)\n int tmp = spatial_idx;\n int ow = tmp % out_width; tmp /= out_width;\n int oh = tmp % out_height; tmp /= out_height;\n int od = tmp % out_depth; tmp /= out_depth;\n int b = tmp;\n \n // For each output channel group, compute all channels\n for (int g = 0; g < groups; ++g) {\n for (int oc_local = 0; oc_local < out_ch_per_group; ++oc_local) {\n int oc = g * out_ch_per_group + oc_local;\n \n float acc = 0.0f;\n \n for (int kd = 0; kd < kernel_d; ++kd) {\n int rem_d = od + padding_d -
